Inclusion of studies comparing patients remitted from depression and controls enabled us to investigate whether cognitive impairment persists beyond episodes of low mood in depression.</jats:p></jats:sec><jats:sec id=\"S0033291713002535_sec_a3\" sec-type=\"results\"><jats:title>Results</jats:title><jats:p>Our meta-analysis revealed significant moderate cognitive deficits in executive function, memory and attention in patients with depression relative to controls (Cohen's <jats:italic>d</jats:italic> effect sizes ranging from \u22120.34 to \u22120.65). Significant moderate deficits in executive function and attention (Cohen's <jats:italic>d</jats:italic> ranging from \u22120.52 to \u22120.61) and non-significant small/moderate deficits in memory (Cohen's <jats:italic>d</jats:italic> ranging from \u22120.22 to \u22120.54) were found to persist in patients whose depressive symptoms had remitted, indicating that cognitive impairment occurs separately from episodes of low mood in depression.</jats:p></jats:sec><jats:sec id=\"S0033291713002535_sec_a4\" sec-type=\"conclusion\"><jats:title>Conclusions</jats:title><jats:p>Both low mood and cognitive impairment are associated with poor psychosocial functioning. Therefore, we argue that remediation of cognitive impairment and alleviation of depressive symptoms each play an important role in improving outcome for patients with depression. "start": {"date-parts": [[2013, 3, 12]], "date-time": "2013-03-12T00:00:00Z", "timestamp": 1363046400000}, "delay-in-days": 0, "content-version": "unspecified"}], "content-domain": {"domain": [], "crossmark-restriction": false}, "short-container-title": ["CNS Spectr."], "published-print": {"date-parts": [[2013, 6]]}, "abstract": "<jats:p>We discuss the importance of cognitive abnormalities in unipolar depression, drawing the distinction between \u201chot\u201d (emotion-laden) and \u201ccold\u201d (emotion-independent) cognition. \u201cCold\u201d cognitive impairments are present reliably in unipolar depression, underscored by their presence in the diagnostic criteria for major depressive episodes. There is good evidence that some \u201ccold\u201d cognitive abnormalities do not disappear completely upon remission, and that they predict poor response to antidepressant drug treatment. However, in many studies the degree of impairment is moderately related to symptoms. We suggest that \u201ccold\u201d cognitive deficits in unipolar depression may in part be explicable in terms of alterations in \u201chot\u201d processing, particularly on tasks that utilize feedback, on which depressed patients have been reported to exhibit a \u201ccatastrophic response to perceived failure.\u201d Other abnormalities in \u201chot\u201d cognition are commonly observed on tasks utilizing emotionally valenced stimuli, with numerous studies reporting mood-congruent processing biases in depression across a range of cognitive domains. Additionally, an emerging literature indicates reliable reward and punishment processing abnormalities in depression, which are especially relevant for hard-to-treat symptoms such as anhedonia. Both emotional and reward biases are strongly influenced by manipulations of the neurochemical systems targeted by antidepressant drugs. Such a pattern of \u201chot\u201d and \u201ccold\u201d cognitive abnormalities is consistent with our cognitive neuropsychological model of depression, which proposes central roles for cognitive abnormalities in the generation, maintenance, and treatment of depressive symptoms. Future work should examine in greater detail the role that \u201chot\u201d and \u201ccold\u201d cognitive processes play in mediating symptomatic improvement following pharmacological, psychological,
